Bequest of a Heir's Share vs. Like a Heir's Share
Explains why bequest of an heir's share is invalid while bequest of 'like' is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a bequest of an heir's share and a bequest of the like of an heir's share, and state the Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
Bequest of an heir's share is invalid as it is another's property; bequest of 'like' it is valid as a separate amount.

A bequest that refers to the share of an heir raises a subtle issue: does the testator own that share at the time of the will? The Hanafi school answers that the heir's share is determined only after death, so it is not the testator's to give. However, if the testator bequeaths 'the like of' the share, it is a separate amount that he does own, making the bequest valid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and a classical dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bequests
Note the two phrases: 'bequest of the share of my son' and 'bequest of the like of the share of my son'. Recognize that the first refers to the actual share the son will inherit, the second to a separate equal amount.
The key difference is the word 'like' (mithl).
2
State the ruling for each
According to the Hanafi school: the first is invalid (batil), the second is valid (sahih) subject to the one-third limit.
Invalid means the bequest fails; valid means it is effective if within the permissible limit.
3
Explain the reasoning
The son's share is what he receives after death, so at the time of the bequest it is not the testator's property. Therefore, bequeathing it is a bequest of another's property, which is invalid. For 'like the share', it is a separate amount that the testator can validly bequeath.
This is based on the principle that a bequest must be of one's own property.
4
Analyze the dispute with Zufar
Zufar argued that the first bequest is also valid via ellipsis (deletion of the annexed noun), interpreting it as 'like the share'. The Hanafi response: ellipsis requires contextual evidence, lacking here. The Quranic example (ask the village) has such evidence; this case does not.
The dispute is over linguistic interpretation and whether deletion is permissible without indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وبنصيب ابنه بطل وبمثل نصيب ابنه صح) أي الوصية بنصيب ابنه في باطلة والوصية بمثل نصيب ابنه صحيحة. وقال زفر : كلتاهما صحيحة لأن الجميع ماله الحال وذكر نصيب الابن للتقدير به، ولأنه يجوز أنه حذف المضاف وأقام المضاف إليه مقامه، فقوله أوصيت بنصيب ابني أي بمثل نصيبه ومثله شائع لغة قال الله تعالى واسأل القرية [يوسف: 82] أي أهلها ولنا أن نصيب الابن ما يصيبه بعد الموت فكان وصية بمال الغير بخلاف ما إذا أوصى بمثل نصيب ابنه لأن مثل الشيء غيره وإنما يجوز حذف المضاف إذا كان هناك ما يدل عليه كما في الآية لأن السؤال يدل على المسؤول وهو الأهل ولم يوجد هنا ما يدل على المحذوف فلا يجوز.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And by the share of his son it is invalid, and by the like of the share of his son it is valid.' That is, the bequest of the share of his son is invalid, and the bequest of the like of the share of his son is valid. Zufar said: Both are valid, because all of it is his property at present, and the mention of the son's share is for estimation by it, and because it is permissible to delete the annexed noun and put the annex in its place, so his saying 'I bequeath the share of my son' means 'the like of his share', and the like is common in language. Allah Almighty said: 'And ask the village' [Yusuf:82], meaning its people. But our argument is that the share of the son is what he obtains after death, so it is a bequest of the property of someone else, unlike if he bequeaths 'the like of the share of his son', because the like of a thing is other than it. Deletion of the annexed noun is only permissible when there is evidence indicating it, as in the verse, because the question indicates the questioned (the people), and here there is no evidence indicating the deleted word, so it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage clarifies a key distinction in bequests: if a testator says 'I bequeath to Zaid the share of my son', this is invalid because the son's share is determined after death and is not the testator's property at the time of bequest—it belongs to the son as an heir. However, if he says 'I bequeath to Zaid the like of the share of my son', this is valid because 'like' indicates a separate amount equal to the son's share, which is within the testator's discretion (within the one-third limit). Imam Zufar argued that both should be valid, interpreting 'share of my son' as meaning 'like of his share' by ellipsis. But the Hanafi position rejects this because ellipsis requires contextual evidence, which is lacking here. The Quranic example (ask the village) is distinguished because the verb 'ask' naturally implies asking people, not buildings. Thus, the ruling stands: bequest of an heir's share is invalid; bequest of the like of it is valid.

Key Terms
نصيب الابن (nasib al-ibn): The actual share that the son receives from the estate after the testator's death.
مثل نصيب الابن (mithl nasib al-ibn): An amount equal to the son's share, but not the same property; it is a separate bequest.
حذف المضاف (hadhf al-mudaf): Ellipsis where the annexed noun is omitted and the annex (genitive) stands for the full construct phrase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says: 'I bequeath the share of my son to Zaid.' What is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) Valid, because the estate is his property
B) Invalid, because it is a bequest of another's property
C) Valid only if the son consents
D) Valid, interpreted as 'like the share'

Key Takeaway
A bequest of an heir's specific share is invalid because it is not the testator's property; a bequest of 'like' the share is valid as it refers to a separate amount.

Bequest of Selling a Slave: The One-Third Beneficiary's Share
Ruling from Abu Yusuf on bequest of slave sale and one-third gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequests involving the sale of a slave and a one-third share, and apply it to analogous cases.

Core Idea
A bequest of one-third does not give the beneficiary a share in the slave's body; only in its price.

Understanding how bequests interact with multiple beneficiaries is crucial. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a specific case: a testator bequeaths the sale of his slave to one person at its value, and one-third of his wealth to another. The ruling clarifies that the one-third beneficiary's claim is limited to a portion of the sale price, not a direct share in the slave itself. This nuance prevents conflicts in estate distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
There are two bequests: one to sell the slave to a named buyer at its value, and another to give one-third of the estate to a different person. Recognize that the slave is an asset of the estate.
The testator specifies the sale price equals the slave's value, so no loss or gain.
2
State the ruling by Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf rules that the one-third beneficiary has no claim on the slave itself. The slave is sold, and the beneficiary receives one-third of the sale price.
This distinguishes between rights to specific property (raqabah) and rights to the value.
3
Understand the reasoning
The bequest of one-third applies to the estate's value, not to specific items. The slave is a specific item designated for sale, so the one-third beneficiary cannot take a portion of it directly.
The ruling prevents conflict: the buyer gets the slave, the one-third beneficiary gets a share of the proceeds.
4
Note the authority
The text states that the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) is well-known, indicating it is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).
This is a Zahir al-Riwayah position, authoritative in the Hanafi school.
5
Apply to analogous cases
Consider a testator bequeathing a specific car to one person and one-third of the estate to another. The ruling implies the one-third beneficiary gets a share of the car's sale price if the car is sold, not the car itself.
The principle applies whenever a bequest of a fraction of the estate coexists with a bequest of a specific item.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أوصى أن يباع من الرجل بألف وهي قيمته ولآخر بثلث ماله قال أبو يوسف : لا شيء لصاحب الثلث من الرقبة ويباع العبد فيكون له ثلث ثمنه وقد ذكرنا هذا فيما أوصى لرجل بجميع ماله وقولهما في هذا معروف .
If he bequeaths that (the slave) be sold to a man for one thousand (dirhams) while it is his value, and to another with one-third of his wealth, Abu Yusuf said: There is nothing for the owner of the one-third from the slave's body, and the slave is sold, and he gets one-third of its price. We have already mentioned this in the case of bequeathing to a man all his wealth, and the statement of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in this is well-known.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a specific case from the Hanafi school regarding bequests. The scenario: a testator bequeaths that his slave be sold to a specific man for 1000 dirhams (which is the slave's actual value), and also bequeaths to another person one-third of his entire wealth. Abu Yusuf rules that the person entitled to one-third does not receive any share in the slave's body itself (i.e., he cannot claim a portion of the slave as part of his one-third). Instead, the slave is sold, and from its sale price, the one-third beneficiary receives one-third of that price. The author notes that this ruling is analogous to an earlier discussion about bequeathing all one's wealth to one person. The known position of both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) on this matter is well-known, implying that this is the relied-upon view within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الرقبة (al-raqabah): The physical body of the slave as a whole, as opposed to its value or price.
ثلث المال (thulth al-mal): One-third of the testator's estate after debts and funeral expenses, which is the maximum for bequests without heirs' permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if a testator bequeaths to sell his slave to Zayd for its value and to 'Amr one-third of his wealth, what does 'Amr receive?
A) One-third of the slave itself
B) One-third of the slave's sale price
C) Nothing
D) Half of the slave

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a bequest of a fraction of the estate does not entitle the beneficiary to a share of a specific asset bequeathed to another; they only receive a share of its sale price.

Bequest to a Mosque: Explicit Expenditure Required?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on bequest to mosque

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding a bequest to a mosque without specifying expenditure, and explain the practical consequence of their difference.

Core Idea
A bequest to a mosque without specifying expenditure is valid per Muhammad, not per Abu Yusuf.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced disagreement within the Hanafi school. The issue is whether simply saying 'to the mosque' suffices for a valid bequest, or whether the testator must explicitly state that the bequest is for expenditure on the mosque. Understanding this difference is crucial for drafting valid wills and appreciating the internal diversity of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is a bequest (wasiyyah) of one-third of wealth to a mosque without specifying how the money should be spent. The dispute is whether such a bequest is valid.
2
State Muhammad's position and reasoning
Imam Muhammad holds it is valid unconditionally. His reasoning: the mosque is a valid recipient; it can own property as a legal entity.
Muhammad's view is part of the zahir al-riwayah.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf holds it is not valid unless the testator says 'to be spent on the mosque'. His reasoning: a mosque as a building cannot own property; the bequest must designate a benefit (expenditure).
Abu Yusuf emphasizes the need for explicit purpose.
4
Note the additional detail from al-Khaniyah
The ruling applies whether the testator specifies a particular mosque or not – the same positions hold.
This expands the scope of the dispute.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Muhammad, the bequest is executed directly – the mosque receives the money. Under Abu Yusuf, without the phrase 'to be spent', the bequest is null, unless it can be interpreted as a bequest for the mosque's benefit (which the text does not allow).
The practical consequence is whether the testator's wish is fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال أوصيت بثلث مالي للمسجد جاز عند محمد. وقال أبو يوسف : لا يجوز إلا أن يقول ينفق على المسجد. وفي الخانية ولو أوصى بثلث ماله للمسجد وعين المسجد أو لم يعين
And if he says: I bequeath one-third of my wealth to the mosque, it is valid according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: It is not valid unless he says '(it is) to be spent on the mosque'. In al-Khaniyah: If he bequeaths one-third of his wealth to the mosque, specifying the mosque or not specifying it...
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding bequests to a mosque. According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, simply saying 'to the mosque' is sufficient for the bequest to be valid; the mosque is a legal entity that can own property. Imam Abu Yusuf requires that the testator explicitly state that the bequest is for expenditure on the mosque, because a mosque as a building cannot own property; it is the mosque administrators who spend on it. The text from al-Khaniyah notes that whether the testator specifies a particular mosque or not, the same ruling applies. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is the view of Muhammad, as it is more in line with common practice, though some later scholars may prefer Abu Yusuf's caution.

Key Terms
المسجد (al-masjid): The mosque as a physical building; in legal terms, it can be a recipient of bequest for its benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, if someone says 'I bequeath one-third of my wealth to the mosque', the bequest is:
A) Void
B) Valid only if a specific mosque is named
C) Valid unconditionally
D) Valid only if expenditure is mentioned

Key Takeaway
A bequest to a mosque without specifying expenditure is valid by the majority Hanafi view (Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf's requirement for explicit expenditure.

Bequests Phrased as 'Like the Share of a Son'
Rulings on wasiyyah with 'like son's share' phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the bequest amount for a wasiyyah expressed as 'mithl nasib al-ibn' in various heir configurations, applying the one-third limit and consent rules.

Core Idea
A bequest 'like my son's share' equals the son's actual share, but is capped at one-third unless heirs consent.

When someone bequeaths 'like the share of my son', the amount depends on how many sons and daughters exist, and whether the heirs approve. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the calculations and the crucial one-third limit. Understanding these rules protects the legatee's right and respects the heirs' entitlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the bequest phrase and number of sons/daughters
Determine the exact wording: 'mithl nasib al-ibn' (like the share of my son) and count the testator's sons and daughters at death.
If there are no sons, the phrase is interpreted as 'like the share of a hypothetical son'.
2
Calculate the nominal share of the bequest
If there is one son, the bequest nominally equals half the estate. If two sons, it equals one-third. For daughters, one daughter = half, two daughters = one-third.
The bequest is compared to the share a single son/daughter would receive.
3
Apply the one-third rule
If the nominal share exceeds one-third of the estate, the bequest is reduced to one-third unless all heirs give posthumous consent (ijazah). If it is one-third or less, no consent is needed.
The one-third limit is a default for wasiyyah; consent allows up to the entire estate.
4
Handle consent scenarios
If the heirs consent, the legatee receives the full nominal share. If they refuse, he receives only one-third.
Consent is only needed when the bequest exceeds one-third.
5
Apply to hypothetical son cases
If there is no son, the bequest is treated as if there were a son. For a brother and sister, a bequest of 'share of a hypothetical son' gives the legatee the whole estate if siblings consent; if not, one-third.
A son would exclude siblings, so the bequest can consume the whole estate with consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان أكثر من فيحتاج إلى إجازة الورثة، فإن كان ثلثاً أو أقل جازت من غير إجازة نحو ما إذا أوصى بمثل نصيب ابنه وله ابن واحد صار موصياً له بنصف المال ولو كان له ابنان يكون بينهما نصفين كذلك ها هنا يكون المال بينهما نصفين نصف للابن ونصف للموصى له إن أجاز الابن، وإن لم يجز الابن فللموصى له الثلث وإن كان له ابنان فإنه يكون للموصى له ثلث المال ولا يحتاج إلى الإجازة. ولو أوصى بمثل نصيب ابنه وله ابنة واحدة فإنه يكون للموصى له نصف المال إن أجازت الابنة، وإن لم تجز فله الثلث. ولو كانت له ابنتان والمسألة بحالها فيكون للموصى له ثلث المال. ولو أوصى بنصيب ابن لو كان فالجواب فميه كما لو أوصى بمثل نصيب ابنه. قال : وإذا هلك الرجل وترك أخاً وأختاً وأوصى لرجل بنصيب ابن لو كان فأجاز فللموصى له المال ولا شيء للأخ والأخت، ولو أوصى بمثل نصيب ابن لو كان للموصى له نصف المال إن أجاز وإن لم يجز فللموصى له ثلث المال إن أجاز أو لم يجز.
Then if it is more than one-third, it requires consent of the heirs. If it is one-third or less, it is permitted without consent, such as when he bequeaths like the share of his son and he has one son, then the legatee gets half of the estate. And if he had two sons, it would be between them half and half, likewise here the estate is between them half and half: half for the son and half for the legatee if the son consents. If the son does not consent, then for the legatee is one-third. And if he has two sons, then for the legatee is one-third of the estate and consent is not needed. And if he bequeaths like the share of his son and he has one daughter, then for the legatee is half of the estate if the daughter consents, and if she does not consent, then for him is one-third. And if he had two daughters, and the case is the same, then for the legatee is one-third of the estate. And if he bequeaths the share of a hypothetical son, then the answer is in it as if he bequeaths like the share of his son. He said: When a man dies leaving a brother and a sister and bequeaths to a man the share of a hypothetical son and they consent, then for the legatee is the entire estate and nothing for the brother and sister. And if he bequeaths like the share of a hypothetical son, for the legatee is half of the estate if they consent, and if they do not consent, then for the legatee is one-third of the estate, whether they consent or not.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings from the Hanafi school on bequests phrased in terms of the shares of heirs. The author explains that a bequest that exceeds one-third requires the consent of the heirs. If it is one-third or less, it is valid without consent. The key example is a bequest 'like the share of my son'. If there is one son, such a bequest effectively gives the legatee half of the estate, but it is only effective in full if the son consents; otherwise, the legatee takes only one-third. If there are two sons, the bequest is automatically one-third (since each son gets half, the bequest of like one son's share is half, but it is limited to one-third without consent; the text says it is one-third without consent, indicating that the bequest is interpreted as one-third of the estate). Similarly, for daughters: one daughter gives half if she consents, else third; two daughters give one-third without consent. The passage also covers bequests phrased as 'the share of a hypothetical son' (i.e., if there were a son). The ruling is the same as for 'like the share of his son'. The specific case of a brother and sister: if the deceased leaves only a brother and sister and bequeaths the share of a hypothetical son, if the siblings consent, the legatee takes the entire estate, because a son would exclude siblings. If they do not consent, the legatee gets one-third.

Key Terms
الموصى له (al-mūṣā lahu): The person who receives the bequest.
الإجازة (al-ijāzah): The consent of the heirs, required for bequests exceeding one-third.
مثل نصيب الابن (mithl naṣīb al-ibn): A bequest phrased as 'like the share of (my) son'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man dies leaving one daughter. He bequeaths 'like the share of my son'. The daughter does not consent. What does the legatee receive?
A) Half the estate
B) One-third of the estate
C) The entire estate
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
A bequest expressed as 'like my son's share' is calculated based on the son's actual inheritance share, but never exceeds one-third of the estate without the consent of all heirs.

Two Bequests and Heir Consent: Calculating Shares in Wasiyyah
Faraid-style calculation for two bequests under various consent scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the shares of two legatees when bequests exceed one-third, under conditions of full, partial, or no consent by the heirs.

Core Idea
Bequests exceeding one-third are proportionally reduced unless heirs consent; consent changes distribution.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a classic Hanafi case of two bequests that together exceed the one-third limit of the estate. The ruling demonstrates how the consent of the heirs (the two sons) alters the shares of the legatees. We will walk through the calculation method for each scenario: no consent, full consent, and one consenting heir.

Guided Path
1
Identify parties and bequests
The deceased left two sons and made two bequests: one for half the estate, another for 'like the share of one son' (i.e., an amount equal to a son's inheritance share). The total bequest exceeds one-third, so heirs consent matters.
2
Apply default rule: no consent
Without consent, both bequests are limited to one-third of the estate. The legatees share that third proportionally according to their claims. The first legatee's claim is half the estate, the second's claim is one-ninth (based on the text's calculation). These fractions are reduced to fit within the third.
The text gives the second legatee's claim as 'one-ninth' — this is likely derived from the ratio of shares.
3
Calculate with full consent
When both sons consent, the bequests are honored in full. The first legatee takes half, the second takes like a son's share (which is one-ninth of the remaining? Or of the whole? The text says the legatees get half the estate together, but calculations are corrupted.
The OCR corruption makes exact numbers uncertain; key is the principle: consent removes the third limit.
4
Analyze one consenting heir
If only one son consents, only his share of the bequest is released. The text says the consenting heir's consent applies to his own inheritance portion, leading to a quarter for that legatee and a third for the other? The exact fractions are garbled but the rule is partial consent yields partial override.
The underlying rule: each heir's consent applies to his own share of the bequest deduction.
5
State the principle
The Hanafi rule: consent of heirs allows bequests beyond one-third; each heir's consent is effective only on his own portion. The calculation of shares must be done proportionally based on the bequest claims and the consent status.
This is derived from the cases in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
روى بشر عن أبي يوسف وفي الأمالي : هلك وترك ابنين وأوصى لرجل بنصف ماله ولآخر بمثل نصيب أحد الابنين ولم تجز الورثة قال : الثلث بين الموصى لهما يضرب فيها صاحب النصف بنصف المال والآخر بتسع المال، فإن أجاز الابنان وصيتهما يأخذ صاحب النصف تمام النصف أربعة ونصفاً من وصاحب مثل النصيب يأخذ سهمين من تسعة ويبقى للابنين تسعان ونصف. ولو كان أوصى لرجل بنصيب أحد الابنين وأوصى لآخر بمثل نصيب الآخر وأجاز الابنان كان لهما نصف المال وللابنين النصف ولو لم يجيزا فالثلث بينهما نصفان وإن أجاز أحدهما دون الآخر فللذي أجاز الربع اعتبار الوجود الإجازة وللذي لم يجز الثلث .
Bishr narrated from Abu Yusuf, and in Al-Amali: A man died leaving two sons and bequeathed to one man half of his wealth and to another man like the share of one of the two sons, and the heirs did not consent. He said: The third is between the two legatees, in which the owner of half strikes with half of the wealth and the other with a ninth of the wealth. Then if the two sons consent to their bequests, the owner of half takes the full half, four and a half from... and the owner of like the share takes two shares from nine, and remains for the two sons two ninths and a half. And if he had bequeathed to a man the share of one of the two sons and to another like the share of the other, and the two sons consented, then they (the legatees) have half of the wealth and for the two sons half. And if they do not consent, then the third between them is half and half. And if one of them consents and the other does not, then for the one who consented is a quarter, considering the existence of consent, and for the one who did not consent is a third.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage records a specific case from the narration of Bishr from Abu Yusuf and from the book Al-Amali. The scenario: a man dies leaving two sons, and makes two bequests: one for half of his wealth, and another for 'like the share of one of the sons'. Since the total bequest (half + something) clearly exceeds one-third, the heirs (the two sons) have the option to consent. If they do not consent, the bequests are reduced to one-third of the estate, and the two legatees share that third proportionally. The text mentions that the first legatee's claim is half and the second's is one-ninth, but this may be a corruption. In the consent scenario, the calculations become more complex, with the estate divided into shares. The passage demonstrates how the shares change based on consent of all, none, or one heir. This is a technical illustration of the interplay between bequests and inheritance shares in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
يضرب (yadrib): In faraid calculations, 'yadrib' means to multiply or to apply a fraction; here it indicates how each legatee's claim is proportioned within the third.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case from Bishr, if the heirs do not consent, what fraction of the estate is allocated to the two legatees?
A) Half
B) One-third
C) Two-thirds
D) The entire estate

Key Takeaway
Without heir consent, bequests exceeding one-third are proportionally reduced to fit within one-third; full or partial consent alters the distribution.

Bequest Calculation with Father and Son as Heirs
Presents detailed faraid arithmetic for a bequest with father and son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the shares of a father, a son, and a legatee when a bequest of 'like the son's share' is made, distinguishing the cases of both consenting, none consenting, and mixed consent.

Core Idea
The legatee's share shifts between 5/11 (consent) and 1/3 (no consent), with intermediate calculations when one heir consents.

In Islamic inheritance, a bequest to a non-heir interacts with the heirs' shares. This lesson examines a specific case where a man leaves a father and a son, and bequeaths to a third party an amount equal to the son's share. The calculation changes dramatically depending on whether the father and son consent to the bequest. We will walk through the arithmetic step by step, following the classical Hanafi method.

Guided Path
1
State the case and identify the heirs
The deceased leaves a father and a son as his only heirs. He also makes a bequest to a third party (legatee) of 'like the share of his son' – i.e., an amount equal to what the son would inherit if there were no bequest.
The bequest is phrased as 'مثل نصيب ابنه' – like the share of his son.
2
Calculate when both heirs consent
If both the father and the son consent to the bequest, the estate is divided into 11 equal parts. The legatee takes 5 parts, the father takes 1 part, and the son takes 5 parts. This yields shares: legatee 5/11, father 1/11, son 5/11.
The base number 11 comes from the fact that the son's share (without bequest) would be 5 parts out of 6 (father 1/6, son 5/6), but consent adjusts the division.
3
Calculate when neither heir consents
If they do not consent, the legatee first takes 1/3 of the entire estate (the maximum bequest without consent). The remaining 2/3 is divided between father and son, with the father taking 1/6 of the whole (i.e., 1/6) and the son taking the remainder (1/2). So shares: legatee 1/3, father 1/6, son 1/2.
In this case, the estate base is 6, but the text uses 9 as the base for convenience. Actually, 1/3 = 3/9, 1/6 = 1.5/9? Wait, the text says 'from nine' – we'll explain.
4
Handle mixed consent using common denominator
When one consents and the other does not, we consider two simultaneous distributions. The text multiplies the two base numbers (11 from consent case and 9 from non-consent case) to get 99 as the common denominator. It then calculates the legatee's shares in each scenario: 45 in consent, 33 in non-consent. The difference (12) is distributed: 2 from the father (his share goes from 9 to 11) and 10 from the son (from 45 to 55).
The exact arithmetic is complex; the key takeaway is the method of combining two fractions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإذا هلك الرجل وترك أباً وابناً وأوصى لرجل بمثل نصيب ابنه أو بنصيب ابن لو كان وأجاز فللموصى له خمسة من أحد عشر وللأب سهم وللابن خمسة، وإن لم يجيز فللموصى له الثلث أولاً والباقي بين الأب والابن أسداساً. وإن أجاز أحدهم دون الآخر وذكر في الكتاب أنه ينظر إلى حال الأجازة وحال عدم الإجازة فالفريضة عند الإجازة من أحد عشر للموصى له خمسة وعند عدم الإجازة الفريضة من تسعة للموصى له ثلثه فيضرب أحد الفريضتين في الأخرى فيصير تسعة وتسعين، فعند عدم الإجازة للموص له الثلث ثلاثة وثلاثون وللأب سدس وما بقي أحد عشر وللابن خمسة من أحد عشر وللابن خمسة أسداس وما بقي خمسة وخمسون وعند الإجازة للموصى له خمسة من أحد عشر مضروباً في تسعة فيكون خمسة وأربعين وللأب سهم مضروباً في تسعة فيكون تسعة فتفاوت ما بين الحالتين في حق الموصى له اثنا عشر سهماً . من ذلك من نصيب الأب وذلك من تسعة إلى أحد عشر وعشرة من نصيب الابن وذلك من خمسة وأربعون إلى خمسة وخمسين، فإذا أجاز ولو قال أوصيت بثلث مالي للمسجد جاز عند محمد.
He said: When a man dies leaving a father and a son and bequeaths to a man like the share of his son, or the share of a hypothetical son, and they consent, then for the legatee is five out of eleven, and for the father is one share, and for the son is five. If they do not consent, then for the legatee is the third first, and the remainder is between the father and the son in sixths. And if one of them consents and the other does not, and it is mentioned in the book that one looks at the case of consent and the case of non-consent. The estate when consent is given is from eleven: for the legatee five; and when consent is not given, the estate is from nine: for the legatee its third. Then one multiplies the two estates into each other, yielding ninety-nine. When consent is not given, for the legatee the third is thirty-three, and for the father a sixth and what remains is eleven, and for the son five out of eleven, and for the son five sixths, and what remains is fifty-five. When consent is given, for the legatee five out of eleven multiplied by nine, so it becomes forty-five, and for the father one share multiplied by nine, becoming nine. So the difference between the two states regarding the legatee is twelve shares. Some of that is from the father's share, that is from nine to eleven, and ten from the son's share, that is from forty-five to fifty-five. So if he consents, and if he says 'I bequeath one-third of my wealth to the mosque', it is valid according to Muhammad.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents a complex faraid calculation regarding a bequest to a third party when the heirs are a father and a son. The bequest is phrased like the share of the son. The calculation shows how the shares change depending on whether the father and son consent. If both consent, the legatee gets 5/11, the father gets 1/11, and the son gets 5/11. If they do not consent, the legatee gets 1/3, and the remaining 2/3 is divided between father and son, with the father taking 1/6 of the whole (so 1/6) and the son taking the remainder (1/2). The math is done by assuming the estate is first divided as a base number (9 or 11) and then the two scenarios are combined to find a common denominator for cases where one consents and the other does not. The numbers given are the results of such a calculation: ninety-nine is the common denominator. The explanation involves finding the difference in shares between consent and non-consent states. This is a technical illustration for those interested in the mathematical details of inheritance combined with bequests. The final sentence then transitions to a new topic about bequests to a mosque.

Key Terms
فريضة (farīḍah): The assumed estate or the base number used in inheritance calculations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 1/11
B) 1/9
C) 1/6
D) 5/11

Key Takeaway
The legatee's share in a bequest of 'like the son's share' is 5/11 when both consent, 1/3 when neither consents, and intermediate calculations apply when consent is mixed.

Bequest to the Mosque: Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on bequest wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imams Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on a bequest to a mosque and identify the condition required by Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A bequest to a mosque is valid only if it specifies expenditure according to Abu Yusuf; Muhammad accepts simple naming.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between two of Abu Hanifa's foremost students. The question: if someone simply says 'I bequeath one-third of my wealth to the mosque,' is it valid? The answer depends on whether the mosque can own property directly or only receive benefits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State clearly what Muhammad and Abu Yusuf each rule.
Muhammad: valid; Abu Yusuf: valid only if expenditure wording.
2
Locate the dispute's basis
The disagreement stems from the legal capacity of a mosque to own property (tamlik vs tasbil).
Abu Yusuf argues mosque cannot own; Muhammad focuses on intended benefit.
3
Apply to similar cases
The same principle extends to bequests for the Kaaba, frontier, etc., as seen in the passage.
This will be detailed in Lesson 2.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال أوصيت بثلث مالي للمسجد جاز عند محمد. وقال أبو يوسف : لا يجوز إلا أن يقول ينفق على المسجد. وفي الخانية ولو أوصى بثلث ماله للمسجد وعين المسجد أو لم يعين فهي باطلة في قول أبي يوسف جائزة في قول محمد . وفي النوازل : إذا أوصى لأرباب المسجد المعين وعمارته وفي ثمن آجر وجبس وغيره فيما احتيج إليه وما كان فيه مصلحة جاز، ولو بجنب هذا المسجد نهر يجري ماؤه بالمسجد ففسد النهر ولم يصل إلى المحلة جاز أن ينفقوا منها في ذلك عند تبين الضرر. وفي العيون عن محمد : إذا قال ثلث مالي للكعبة جاز ويعطى مساكين مكة، ولو قال لثغور فلان فالقياس أن يبطل، وفي الاستحسان يجوز . الظهيرية : ولو قال لبيت المقدس جاز وينفق عليه :سراجه : قيل هذا في عرفهم ولو أوصى بثلث ماله يسرج به في المسجد يجوز ، ولو أوصى بثلث ماله للسراج لا يجوز وهو نظير ما لو أوصى بدرهم لشاة فلان أو بردون فلان فإنه لا يجوز. ولو أوصى بثلث ماله ليعلف به دواب فلان يجوز ونظيره لو أوصى بثلث ماله في أكفان فقراء المسلمين يجوز، ولو أوصى بثلث ماله لموتى الفقراء لا يجوز.
And if he said 'I bequeath one-third of my wealth to the mosque,' it is valid according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: It is not valid unless he says 'to be spent on the mosque.' And in Al-Khaniyah: If he bequeaths one-third of his wealth to the mosque, whether he specified the mosque or not, it is invalid according to Abu Yusuf, valid according to Muhammad. In Al-Nawazil: If he bequeaths to the people of a specified mosque and its upkeep, and for the price of bricks, plaster, and other things that are needed and that benefit it, it is valid. And if next to this mosque there is a river whose water flows to the mosque, and the river breaks and does not reach the locality, it is permissible for them to spend from it in that when the harm becomes evident. In Al-'Uyun from Muhammad: If he says 'one-third of my wealth is for the Kaaba,' it is valid, and it is given to the poor of Mecca. And if he says 'for such-and-such frontier,' then by analogical reasoning it is invalid, but by istihsan it is valid. In Al-Zahiriya: And if he says 'for Jerusalem,' it is valid and to be spent on it. In Siraj: It is said that this is in their custom. And if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth for lighting to be used in the mosque, it is valid. But if he bequeaths it for the lamp itself, it is invalid, and this is similar to if he bequeaths a dirham for so-and-so's sheep or so-and-so's horse, then it is invalid. And if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth for feeding so-and-so's animals, it is valid. And similarly, if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth for shrouds of poor Muslims, it is valid. But if he bequeaths one-third for dead poor people, it is invalid.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the validity of a bequest to a mosque. Abu Hanifa's view is not explicitly stated here. According to Muhammad, a bequest simply stating 'to the mosque' is valid, and the estate should be used for the mosque's benefit and upkeep. According to Abu Yusuf, such a bequest is invalid unless it is expressly stated 'to be spent on the mosque' (ينفق على المسجد), because a mosque as a physical structure cannot own property; rather, the benefit should be the objective. The passage then provides further rulings from various compilations like Al-Khaniyah, Al-Nawazil, Al-'Uyun, and Al-Zahiriya, extending the principle to bequests for the Kaaba, frontier posts (thughūr), Jerusalem, and specific uses like lighting, feeding animals, and shrouds. The key distinction is whether the bequest is for a specific tangible object that can be owned or for a purpose (like feeding, lighting) that benefits the object or specific beneficiaries. For example, bequesting for lighting in the mosque is valid because it is a use, but bequesting for 'the lamp' itself is invalid because it is a specific item whose ownership is uncertain. Similarly, bequesting for the shroud of poor Muslims is valid because it is a known purpose, but for 'dead poor people' it is invalid because the recipients are unspecified and non-existent at the time of bequest. These rulings are based on istihsan (juristic preference) over strict qiyas, as mentioned for the frontier case.

Key Terms
المسجد (al-masjid): The mosque as a place of worship; as a beneficiary of a bequest, it is not a legal entity that can own property directly.
الاستحسان (al-istihsān): Juristic preference; used here to rule in favor of validity for frontier bequest despite qiyas suggesting invalidity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what condition is required for a bequest to a mosque to be valid?
A) The mosque must be specified
B) The bequest must be phrased as 'to be spent on the mosque'
C) The bequest must be for the imam
D) The bequest must be less than one-third

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school is divided on whether a simple bequest to a mosque is valid, with Abu Yusuf requiring explicit expenditure wording and Muhammad accepting it.

Bequests to Purposes vs. Objects: Hanafi Rulings
Extension of the mosque bequest principle to other cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid bequests based on whether the bequest is for a purpose (benefit) or for a specific object, using examples from the text.

Core Idea
A bequest is valid for a purpose but invalid for a specific object.

This lesson extends the principle from the mosque dispute to a series of analogous cases. The key is whether the bequest is directed to a benefit (manfa'ah) that can be realized, or to a tangible asset that cannot be owned or is uncertain. These rulings are based on istihsān (juristic preference) which overrides strict qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operative principle
The common thread: bequest must be for a purpose (benefit) not for a specific object.
This is derived from comparing the cases.
2
Classify each example
For each, decide: is it for a purpose (valid) or for an object (invalid)?
Use the template: purpose = valid; object = invalid.
3
Explain the role of istihsān
In the frontier example, qiyas says invalid, but istihsān says valid due to custom and need.
This shows Hanafi flexibility.

Solving Complex Bequest Calculations in Hanafi Faraid
Complex wasiyyah calculation with fractions and exception clauses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of solving a complex bequest (wasiyyah) calculation by converting all components into shares and performing iterative adjustments.

Core Idea
Complex bequests are solved by converting all amounts to a common share unit and adjusting via successive remainders.

This lesson explores a sophisticated faraid calculation from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq involving a bequest with multiple fractions and a dirham. Although the text is partially corrupted, the Hanafi method is clear: convert everything to shares, then iteratively adjust to satisfy the bequest and the heirs' shares. We will walk through the steps using a simplified model.

Guided Path
1
Identify all bequest components
List every element of the bequest: a share of an heir's portion, a dirham, fractions (e.g., one-third and one-quarter) of what remains after the bequest from the third of the estate.
In the source: 'like the share of one of them, a dirham, one-third and one-quarter of what remains of the third.'
2
Convert all components to shares
Set a base number of shares for the third of the estate. Convert each component into an equivalent number of shares: assign a fixed share value for the dirham, and treat the fractions as shares of the remaining shares.
The author says 'make the third of the estate into shares' and 'if there is a dirham, make a dirham for each share' to unify the units.
3
Calculate the remainder after initial distribution
Start with the total shares in the third, allocate the bequest parts stepwise, and compute what remains. In the source: 'if two go from fourteen, twelve remain' etc.
The iterative subtraction yields successive remainders.
4
Adjust for the final remainder
The final remainder (surplus from the bequest shares) is returned to the third of the estate or to the heirs, as the case demands. This adjustment may require multiplying and subtracting to find the true base.
The source shows multiple trials (errors) and corrections to find the correct third of the estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو أوصى بمثل نصيب أحدهما وثلث ما ربع بقي ودرهم للآخر، وصورة المسألة : رجل مات وترك ثلاث بنين وأوصى بمثل نصيب أحدهم ودرهم وثلث وربع ما بقي من الثلث فيجعل ثلث المال سهاماً، ولو أحاط بالنصيب سهماً وبالدراهم سهماً لأنه متى كان في الوصية درهم يجعل لكل سهم درهم حتى يصير الحساب كله جنساً واحداً فإذا ذهب اثنان من أربعة عشر يبقى اثنا عشر فأعط بالثلث سهمين وبربعه سبعة يبقى خمسة فأعط بالدرهم الأخر سهماً يبقى أربعة فهذه فاضلة عن سهام الوصايا ترد إلى الورثة فرده إلى ثلث المال فيصير أربعة وثلاثين وحاجتنا إلى ستة لأنا لو أعطينا بالنصيب سهمين فيجب أن يكون نصيب الابنين ستة والخطأ الثاني وقع بزيادة مائة وعشرين والأول بزيادة تسعة وعشرين فاضرب الثلث الأول في الثاني وهو ثمانية وعشرون يصير أربعمائة وخمسة وثلاثين ثم اطرح الأقل من الأكثر يبقى ثلاثة وأربعون فهذا هو الثلث، وإذا اردت معرفة النصيب فاضرب النصيب الأول وهو سهم في المقطع الثاني وهو ثمانية وعشرون فيصير ثمانية وخمسين ثم اطرح الأكثر من الأقل يبقى ثلاثون فظهر عند النصيب ثلاثون وثلث المال ثلاثة وأربعون فيعطى بالنصيب من ثلاثين يبقى ثلاثة عشر فيعطى بالدراهم سهم يبقى اثنا عشر سهماً فيعطى ثلث ما بقي وربعه سبعة بقي خمسة فيعطى من الدرهم الأخر سهم يبقى أربعة فرده الأربعة إلى ثلثي المال وبيان تعليله في المحيط . وأما لو أوصى بمثل نصيب الابن إلا ثلث ما بقي من الثلث صورتها : ترك ثلاث بنين وأوصى لرجل بمثل نصيب أحدهم إلا ثلث ما يبقى من بعد النصيب فالفريضة تسعة وثلاثون والثلث ثلاثة عشر والنصيب بعد الابنين شبهة وبيان تخريجه في المحيط. وأما لو قال في المسألة المتقدمة إلا ثلث ما يبقى من الثلث بعد الوصية فأصل الفريضة ماذكرنا في الفصل الأول. وأما لو قال في صورة المسألة إلا ثلث ما بقي الثلث
If he bequeathed like the share of one of them and one-third of what remains, a quarter of what remains, and a dirham to another, the picture of the case: A man died leaving three sons and bequeathed like the share of one of them, a dirham, one-third and one-quarter of what remains of the third. So one makes the third of the estate into shares. If one designates one share for the share and one share for the dirhams, because whenever there is a dirham in the bequest, one makes a dirham for each share until the account becomes all one type. Then if two go from fourteen, twelve remain. So he gives for the third two shares, and for its quarter seven, leaving five. So he gives for the other dirham one share, leaving four. These are surplus from the shares of the bequests that return to the heirs. So one returns it to the third of the estate, becoming thirty-four. Our need is for six, because if we gave for the share two shares, then the share of the two sons should be six. The second error increased by one hundred and twenty, and the first increased by twenty-nine. So multiply the first third by the second, which is twenty-eight, yielding four hundred and thirty-five. Then subtract the lesser from the greater, leaving forty-three. So this is the third. If you want to know the share, multiply the first share, which is one share, by the second divisor, which is twenty-eight, becoming fifty-eight. Then subtract the greater from the lesser, leaving thirty. So the share turns out to be thirty, and the third of the estate is forty-three. He is given for the share from thirty, leaving thirteen. He is given for the dirhams one share, leaving twelve shares. He is given one-third of what remains and its quarter, seven, leaving five. He is given from the other dirham one share, leaving four. He returns the four to the two-thirds of the estate. The explanation of its reasoning is in Al-Muhit. As for if he bequeathed like the share of the son except one-third of what remains of the third, its form: He leaves three sons and bequeaths to a man like the share of one of them except one-third of what remains after the share. The estate is thirty-nine, the third is thirteen, and the share after the two sons is something? And its derivation is in Al-Muhit. As for if he said in the preceding case 'except one-third of what remains of the third after the bequest', then the base of the estate is what we mentioned in the first chapter. As for if he said in the form of the case 'except one-third of what remains of the third...'
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a highly technical faraid calculation involving a bequest that includes a share, a dirham, and fractional parts of what remains after the third. The author presents a method for solving such complex bequests by converting all amounts into a common unit (shares) and finding the base denominator. The calculation involves successive subtraction and multiplication to determine the actual shares for each component. The passage references Al-Muhit for the detailed reasoning. It also introduces an exception clause (إلا) in the bequest phrased as 'like the share of the son except one-third of what remains'. These are advanced topics for those studying the mathematics of Islamic inheritance (al-fara'id al-wasiyyah). Due to the corrupt nature of the OCR, the exact numbers and steps may not be perfectly recoverable, but the structural method is evident. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi school's meticulous approach to ensuring precise distribution of estates in accordance with the bequest and shares of heirs.

Key Terms
ثلث المال (thulth al-māl): One-third of the estate, the maximum that can be bequeathed without heir consent.
سهم (sahm): Share; a unit used to represent portions of the estate in faraid calculations.
وصية (wasiyyah): Bequest; a gift from the estate to a non-heir, limited to one-third unless heirs consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Why does the Hanafi method in this lesson convert all bequest components into a common share unit?
A) To comply with a requirement that every bequest must be in multiples of the dirham
B) To allow consistent arithmetic addition and subtraction across different types of components
C) To ensure that the bequest does not exceed one-third of the estate
D) To simplify the distribution to the heirs

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses a systematic conversion to shares and iterative adjustment to solve complex bequest calculations, ensuring precise and legally compliant distribution.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Vague Bequests
Intra-Hanafi dispute on vague bequests

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on bequests for unspecified pious purposes and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A general bequest for a good cause is invalid per Abu Yusuf but valid per Muhammad.

This lesson unpacks the first ruling in this passage: a bequest of one-third of one's wealth for an unspecified pious purpose. The two leading Hanafi imams disagree – Abu Yusuf holds it void, Muhammad upholds it. Understanding their divergent reasoning reveals how Hanafi law balances textual strictness with customary flexibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed act
The bequest is of one-third of wealth for a general pious purpose (e.g., 'for the mosque') without specifying a particular institution or individual.
2
State Abu Yusuf's ruling
He rules it invalid (batil). His reasoning: lack of specification – it is like bequeathing to an undefined category, which is void.
3
State Muhammad's ruling
He rules it valid (ja'iz). His reasoning: custom ('urf) and public interest allow it; istihsan overrides strict qiyas that would require specification.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Yusuf, the bequest is void and the one-third remains with the heirs; under Muhammad, it must be executed for the intended purpose.
This practical difference affects whether the bequest is carried out.
5
Note the later tarjih
Although the passage does not specify, later Hanafi fatwa (e.g., in Bahr al-Ra'iq itself) tends to follow Muhammad's view for public benefits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوصايا باب الوصية بثلث المال
فهي باطلة في قول أبي يوسف جائزة في قول محمد . ولو أوصى بأن ينفق ثلثه على المسجد جاز في قولهم. وفي النوازل : إذا أوصى لأرباب المسجد المعين وعمارته وفي ثمن آجر وجبس وغيره فيما احتيج إليه وما كان فيه مصلحة جاز، ولو بجنب هذا المسجد نهر يجري ماؤه بالمسجد ففسد النهر ولم يصل إلى المحلة جاز أن ينفقوا منها في ذلك عند تبين الضرر. وفي العيون عن محمد : إذا قال ثلث مالي للكعبة جاز ويعطى مساكين مكة، ولو قال لثغور فلان فالقياس أن يبطل، وفي الاستحسان يجوز . الظهيرية : ولو قال لبيت المقدس جاز وينفق عليه وفي :سراجه : قيل هذا في عرفهم ولو أوصى بثلث ماله يسرج به في المسجد يجوز ، ولو أوصى بثلث ماله للسراج لا يجوز وهو نظير ما لو أوصى بدرهم لشاة فلان أو بردون فلان فإنه لا يجوز. ولو أوصى بثلث ماله ليعلف به دواب فلان يجوز ونظيره لو أوصى بثلث ماله في أكفان فقراء المسلمين يجوز، ولو أوصى بثلث ماله لموتى الفقراء لا يجوز
The Book of Bequests, Chapter on Bequest of One-Third of the Property:
It is invalid in the opinion of Abu Yusuf, valid in the opinion of Muhammad. And if he bequeaths that his one-third be spent on the mosque, it is valid in their opinion. In al-Nawazil: If he bequeaths to the people of a specific mosque and its upkeep, and for the price of bricks, plaster, and other things needed and that which is beneficial, it is valid. And if next to this mosque there is a river whose water flows to the mosque, and the river becomes spoiled and the water does not reach the mosque, it is permissible to spend from it (the bequest) for that upon the appearance of harm. In al-Uyun from Muhammad: If he says 'one-third of my wealth for the Kaaba', it is valid and is given to the poor of Mecca. And if he says 'for such-and-such a frontier fort', analogically it is invalid, but by istihsan it is valid. Al-Dhahiriyyah: And if he says 'for Bayt al-Maqdis (Jerusalem)', it is valid and is spent on it. And in Siraj: It is said that this is according to their custom. And if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth to provide lamps in the mosque, it is valid. And if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth for the lamp itself, it is not valid, analogous to bequeathing a dirham for so-and-so's sheep or so-and-so's horse, which is not valid. And if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth to feed so-and-so's animals, it is valid; similarly, if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth for shrouds of poor Muslims, it is valid. And if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth for dead poor people, it is not valid.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage from the Bahr al-Ra'iq commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a series of rulings regarding bequests (wasiyyah) of one-third of one's wealth for specific purposes. It opens with an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf considers a bequest for an unspecified purpose invalid, while Muhammad considers it valid. The author then provides examples of valid bequests: upkeep of a specific mosque, including necessary construction materials; if the mosque's water supply fails, the bequest can be used to remedy that. Bequest for the Kaaba is valid but given to the poor of Mecca. For frontier forts (thughur), qiyas would invalidate but istihsan allows it. Bequest for Jerusalem is valid. There is a distinction between bequeathing for lamps (valid) vs. for the lamp itself (invalid) – the latter is like bequeathing for a specific animal owned by someone else, which lacks specificity. Bequest for feeding animals or shrouds for poor Muslims is valid, but for dead poor (i.e., burial expenses that are not a living need) is invalid. The passage shows the Hanafi method of using istihsan to override qiyas in cases of public benefit and custom.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A Hanafi method of legal reasoning that prefers a more flexible ruling over strict qiyas based on necessity, custom, or public interest.
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A bequest or will made during lifetime, taking effect after death, limited to one-third of the estate unless heirs consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Hanafi imam considers a bequest for an unspecified pious purpose invalid?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad

Key Takeaway
A bequest for an unspecified pious purpose is subject to intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: invalid per Abu Yusuf, valid per Muhammad (later relied upon).

Valid Bequests for Religious and Public Institutions
Rulings on bequests for religious and military institutions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the validity of bequests for specified religious institutions and identify the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas for frontier forts.

Core Idea
Bequests for mosques, Kaaba, border forts, and Jerusalem are valid, with istihsan overriding strict analogy for public benefit.

This lesson examines several examples from the passage: bequests for mosque upkeep, for the Kaaba, for frontier forts, and for Jerusalem. These illustrate the Hanafi principle that bequests for specific public benefit are valid, even if analogy might suggest otherwise, and istihsan is used to achieve the intended good.

Guided Path
1
Identify the institutions
The passage mentions mosque (including its maintenance), Kaaba, frontier forts (thughur), and Bayt al-Maqdis (Jerusalem).
2
Note the ruling for each
Bequests for these institutions are all valid (ja'iz).
The mosque bequest includes specific details like bricks and plaster.
3
Understand the frontier fort case
For frontier forts, qiyas would invalidate the bequest (because it's not a person), but istihsan allows it due to public benefit.
This shows istihsan overriding qiyas.
4
Observe the custom argument
For Jerusalem, the passage notes that validity is according to their custom ('urf).
Custom can influence the validity of such bequests.
5
Extract the principle
Bequests for specified public institutions are valid because they serve a living, ongoing benefit.

Specificity in Bequest Wording: Valid vs Invalid Examples
Distinction between valid and invalid bequest wordings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid bequest wordings and apply the principle of specificity to new cases.

Core Idea
A bequest must specify a purpose that benefits living persons; bequests for inanimate objects or deceased individuals are invalid.

The passage makes fine distinctions: bequeathing for lamps in the mosque is valid, but for the lamp itself is invalid; bequeathing for shrouds of poor Muslims is valid, but for dead poor is invalid; bequeathing to feed animals is valid, but for a specific animal owned by someone else is invalid. These rulings reveal the requirement that the bequest must be for a meaningful, ongoing benefit (manfa'ah) to living beings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the valid examples
Valid: bequest for lamps in the mosque (providing a recurring benefit), feeding so-and-so's animals (ongoing maintenance), shrouds for poor Muslims (benefit to living poor for their burial).
2
Identify the invalid examples
Invalid: bequest for the lamp itself (an object), for dead poor (deceased persons), for a specific sheep or horse owned by another (chattel).
3
Extract the principle
Valid bequests are for a purpose that provides an ongoing benefit (manfa'ah) to living persons; invalid ones are for non-living objects or deceased individuals without a living beneficiary.
The invalid examples involve something that cannot receive ownership or benefit.
4
Note the analogy
Bequest for the lamp itself is analogous to bequest for a specific animal – both are invalid because they are specific chattels, not purposes.
5
Apply the principle
For any new case, ask: Does the bequest specify a purpose that will benefit living beings? If yes, it is valid; if it is for an object or deceased, it is invalid.

Source Text

There is a distinction between bequeathing for lamps (valid) vs. for the lamp itself (invalid) – the latter is like bequeathing for a specific animal owned by someone else, which lacks specificity. Bequest for feeding animals or shrouds for poor Muslims is valid, but for dead poor (i.e., burial expenses that are not a living need) is invalid. The passage shows the Hanafi method of using istihsan to override qiyas in cases of public benefit and custom.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A Hanafi method of legal reasoning that prefers a more flexible ruling over strict qiyas based on necessity, custom, or public interest.
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A bequest or will made during lifetime, taking effect after death, limited to one-third of the estate unless heirs consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a valid bequest?
A) One-third for a specific sheep
B) One-third for the lamp itself
C) One-third for lamps in the mosque
D) One-third for dead poor

Key Takeaway
Bequests are valid when they specify a purpose benefitting living beings; bequests for specific objects or the deceased are invalid.

Complex Bequests: Fractions and Residual Shares in Hanafi Law
Computational example of a multi-fraction bequest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate a bequest that includes a share equivalent to a son's share plus dirhams and fractions of the residual one-third, using the Hanafi method of converting all units to shares and solving through error correction.

Core Idea
Convert dirhams to shares and use sequential subtraction and multiplication to resolve bequests with fractions of residuals.

Imagine a testator leaves three sons and bequeaths to one person the equivalent of one son's share, plus a dirham, plus one-third and one-fourth of what remains after taking the one-third of the estate. How do we distribute? The Hanafi school developed a systematic arithmetic method: first convert dirhams into abstract shares, then repeatedly apply fractions to the residual until all components are accounted for. This lesson walks through that calculation from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Bequest Components
Read the passage and list every element of the bequest: (1) equivalent of one son's share (mithl nasib ahadihima), (2) a dirham (dirham), (3) one-third of what remains of the one-third (thuluth ma baqi min al-thuluth), (4) one-fourth of what remains of the one-third (rub' ma baqi min al-thuluth). Note that the testator's estate is divided into two portions: the one-third (max bequest limit) and the remaining two-thirds (for heirs). The bequest is to be satisfied within that one-third.
The 'one-third' here refers to the one-third of the entire estate, not the residual.
2
Convert All Values to Shares
To simplify, represent the one-third of the estate as a number of abstract shares (sahm). Also convert the dirhams into shares: assume each dirham equals one share. This makes the entire calculation homogeneous. In the example, the text says 'two out of fourteen go' – meaning after converting, there are 14 shares initially, and 2 are removed (the dirhams? need to follow the text). Actually the text says 'when two out of fourteen go, twelve remain' suggesting an initial division of the one-third into 14 shares, then subtracting 2 (maybe for the son's share? Let's follow the commentary). The guided path should not paraphrase the specific numbers but guide the student to replicate the method.
The specific numbers may be error-prone due to OCR; focus on the procedure.
3
Apply Fractions to the Residual
After setting the base shares, the bequest has fractions of what remains after previous deductions. In the example: give for one-third of the residual two shares, for one-fourth seven shares, then subtract from the current total. This is done iteratively: first subtract the son's share, then subtract the dirham, then apply the fractions one by one to the remaining shares.
The arithmetic involves careful subtraction in order. The text shows: after initial subtraction, 'give for the one-third two shares and for its one-fourth seven, leaving five'. This implies the residual after the son's share and first dirham was 12, then taking 2+7=9 leaves 3? But text says 5. There's a discrepancy. We'll note the need for verification.
4
Correct for Errors (First and Second Error)
The Hanafi method uses a double false position approach. The 'first error' and 'second error' refer to discrepancies between assumed and actual shares. The text states: 'The second error occurred by an increase of one hundred and twenty, and the first by an increase of twenty-nine.' Then it instructs to multiply the first one-third by the second divisor (28) and subtract. This is a classic algorithm to solve for the correct one-third value. The student should understand that this is a systematic way to adjust an initial guess.
The numbers 29 and 120 are derived from the calculations; the student should focus on the multiplication and subtraction steps.
5
Obtain Final Shares and Distribution
After the error correction, the correct value for the one-third of the estate is found (43 shares), and the share equivalent to one son's share is 30 shares. Then the distribution is straightforward: give 30 to the bequest for the son's share, then one share for the first dirham, then apply the fractions to the remaining 12: one-third of 12 is 4? Actually text says 'give one-third of what remains and its one-fourth, seven' – that is 1/3 + 1/4 = 7/12 of the residual, leaving 5. Then give one share for the second dirham, leaving 4 shares which return to the heirs (added to the two-thirds). Thus the bequest consumes 30+1+7+1 = 39 shares of the 43 one-third? No, 30+1+7+1=39, leaving 4 surplus returned to heirs. That matches the final step.
The surplus shares are returned to the heirs' portion (two-thirds). This illustrates the integration of bequest and inheritance.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents intricate calculations for bequests involving fractions of the one-third portion of the estate. The scenario involves a man leaving three sons and bequeathing to one person the equivalent of the share of one son, plus a dirham, plus one-third and one-fourth of what remains after taking the one-third of the estate. The author describes a step-by-step method: first, the one-third of the estate is divided into shares. Another variation mentions 'except one-third of what remains of the one-third after the bequest.' The author refers to al-Muhit for detailed explanation. These examples demonstrate the Hanafi method of solving complex share problems involving multiple fractions, residuals, and the principle of treating money as shares.

Key Terms
الثلث (al-thuluth): One-third of the testator's estate, which is the maximum limit for bequests without heirs' consent.
السهم (al-sahm): A unit share in the division of the estate, used here as an abstract unit to facilitate calculations.

Meaning of 'Sahm' in Bequests and the Heirs' Delegation
Definition of 'sahm' and delegation to heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of 'sahm' in a bequest, explain the delegation to heirs, and apply the rule that a later bequest supersedes an earlier one.

Core Idea
A bequest of 'a share' is valid but ambiguous; its meaning is determined by default (one-sixth) or by the heirs' discretion.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines the term 'sahm' (share) used in bequests. The Hanafi school holds that such bequests are valid despite ambiguity, and the heirs interpret the testator's intent. The passage also clarifies how successive bequests interact, and compares the bequest of an unknown amount to an acknowledgment of an unknown debt.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default rule for ambiguous bequests
If a testator bequeaths 'a share' or 'a part' of his wealth, it is ambiguous but valid. The heirs are entrusted to explain it and may give whatever they wish, because they stand in the testator's place.
This delegation relies on the principle that the heirs are the testator's successors.
2
Map the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the meaning of 'sahm'
Identify the four reported views: (1) Abu Hanifah: sahm = one-sixth (from Ibn Mas'ud). (2) Al-Jami' al-Saghir: least heir's share but not less than one-sixth. (3) Al-Asl: least heir's share but not more than one-sixth. (4) Al-Hidayah: prevents increase. Note how the commentator reconciles by referring to custom.
Custom ('urf) later shifted to mean the least heir's share, not one-sixth.
3
Apply the ruling on successive bequests
If a testator first bequeaths one-sixth then later says one-third for the same person, the later bequest supersedes. The legatee receives one-third, not one-sixth.
This follows the principle that the later declaration of intent overrides the earlier.
4
Distinguish from acknowledgment of an unknown debt
Acknowledge an unknown debt creates a right for the creditor during the debtor's life. If the debtor dies without specifying, the right may lapse and heirs are not forced to specify, unlike a bequest which becomes effective only after death.
The later scholar's refutation uses qiyas ma'a al-fariq (analogy with a difference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسهم أو جزء من ماله فالبيان إلى الورثة أي إذا أوصى بسهم أو جزء من ماله كان بيان ذلك إلى الورثة فيقال لهم اعطوه ما شئتم لأنه مجهول يتناول القليل والكثير والوصية لا تمتنع بالجهالة والورثة قائمون مقام الموصي فكان إليهم بيانه. سوى هنا بين السهم والجزء وهو اختيار بعض المشايخ والمروي عن أبي حنيفة أن السهم عبارة عن السدس نقل ذلك عن ابن مسعود وعن إياس بن معاذ. وقال في الجامع الصغير : له أخس سهام الورثة إلا أن يكون أقل من السدس فحينئذ يعطى له السدس. وقال في الأصل له أخس سهام الورثة إلا أن يكون أكثر من السدس فلا يزاد عليه جعل السهم يمنع النقصان. وذكر في الهداية أنه يمنع لزيادة ثم قال في تعليقه : إنه يذكر ويراد به السدس ويذكر ويراد به سهم من سهام الورثة لأن السهم يراد به نصيب أحد الورثة عرفاً لا سيما في الوصية فيصرف إليه وهذا في عرفهم، وأما في عرفنا فهو الذي ذكرناه أولاً . قوله ويجزئ» قال صاحب التسهيل أقول : دلت هذه المسألة على أن أحداً لو أقر بمجهول كقوله لفلان على دين ولم يبين قدره فمات مجهلاً تجبر ورثته على البيان، وكذا لو أقيم البينة على إقراره بمجهول ينبغي أن قبل وتجبر ورثته على البيان اهـ ورد عليه بعض المتأخرين حيث قال بعد نقل ذلك قلت : ما ذكره قياس مع الفارق لأن الإقرار ولو بمجهول يوجب تعلق الغير به من وقت الإقرار فيجبر المقر على بيانه بطلب المقر له، فإذا فات الخبر في حياته بوفاته سقط سيما إذا كان بتقصير من المقر له فلم تنب عنه ورثته بخلاف الوصية بمجهول لعدم ثبوت حق الغير إلا بعد موت الموصي فقبل موته لا يجبر على بيانه وبعد موته تعلق الحق بتركته ولا يمكن جبره فيجبر من يقوم مقامه أحياء الحق ثابت قال رحمه الله : (قال) سدس مالي لفلان ثم قال ثلث مالي له له ثلث ماله
And 'a share' or 'a part of his wealth' – its explanation is upon the heirs. That is, if he bequeaths a share or a part of his wealth, its explanation is upon the heirs. So they are told: give him whatever you wish because it is unknown, encompassing little and much, and a bequest is not prevented by ambiguity, and the heirs stand in the place of the testator, so its explanation is upon them. He equated here between share and part, and this is the choice of some shaykhs. And it is reported from Abu Hanifah that 'share' means one-sixth, transmitted from Ibn Mas'ud and from Iyas ibn Mu'adh. And he said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: He gets the least of the shares of the heirs unless it is less than one-sixth, then he is given one-sixth. And he said in al-Asl: He gets the least of the shares of the heirs unless it is more than one-sixth, then he is not increased beyond it, meaning the share prevents decrease. And it is mentioned in al-Hidayah that it prevents increase. Then he said in his commentary: It is mentioned and intended to mean one-sixth, and it is mentioned and intended to mean a share of the heirs' shares, because 'share' is customarily used to mean the portion of one of the heirs, especially in bequests, so it is applied to that, and this according to their custom. As for our custom, it is what we mentioned first. His saying 'and it suffices' – the author of al-Tashil said: I say: This case indicates that if someone makes an acknowledgment of an unknown thing, like saying 'so-and-so has a debt against me' without specifying its amount, and he dies without clarifying, his heirs are forced to clarify. Similarly, if evidence is established of his acknowledgment of an unknown thing, it should be accepted and his heirs forced to clarify. But some later scholars refuted him, saying after quoting that: What he mentioned is qiyas with a difference because an acknowledgment, even if of an unknown thing, establishes a right of another from the time of acknowledgment, so the acknowledger is forced to clarify at the request of the acknowledged. If the news is lost during his lifetime due to his death, it falls, especially if there was negligence from the acknowledged, so his heirs do not stand in his place. Unlike a bequest of an unknown thing, because the right of another is not established except after the testator's death, so before his death he is not forced to clarify, and after his death the right is attached to his estate and he cannot be forced, so those who stand in his place are forced to preserve the established right. He (the author) said: If he said 'one-sixth of my wealth for so-and-so', then said 'one-third of my wealth for him', he gets one-third of his wealth.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with two issues: first, the meaning of 'sahm' (share) or 'juz'' (part) in a bequest, and second, the ruling on successive bequests. The author explains that if someone bequeaths 'a share' or 'a part' of his wealth, it is ambiguous, but the heirs must explain it—they can give whatever they wish. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the definition of 'sahm'. Abu Hanifah is reported to say it means one-sixth (the minimum share of an heir in inheritance). In al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is the least share of the heirs but not less than one-sixth. In al-Asl, it is the least share but not more than one-sixth. Al-Hidayah mentions it prevents increase. The commentator reconciles these by noting that 'sahm' can refer to one-sixth in some contexts, or to a share of the heirs in customary usage, especially in bequests. The passage then discusses whether akin to bequests of unknown amounts, acknowledgments of unknown debts require the heirs to specify. The later scholar refutes equating the two because bequests only take effect after death, while acknowledgments create a right during life; if the debtor dies without specifying, the right falls. Finally, the matn ruling: if a testator says 'one-sixth for so-and-so' then later says 'one-third for him', the later bequest supersedes and he gets one-third, because the later intention supersedes the earlier.

Key Terms
السهم (al-sahm): An ambiguous term in bequests; either one-sixth of the estate or the least share of an heir (depending on interpretation).
البيان (al-bayan): The clarification or specification of an ambiguous bequest, delegated to the heirs after the testator's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One-sixth
B) One-third
C) One-ninth
D) Nothing, because the bequests cancel out

Key Takeaway
A bequest of 'a share' is valid in Hanafi law; its amount is determined by the heirs' discretion or by default rules drawn from transmitted reports and custom.

Bequest with Two Sons: Qiyas Overridden
Ruling on bequest share with two sons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a bequest of a son's share when the testator has two sons and explain why qiyas is overridden.

Core Idea
With two sons, a bequest of a son's share gives one-third, not half — because parity with each son yields a third.

In bequests, the testator may leave a share 'like that of one of my sons.' When there are two sons, the initial analogical reasoning suggests the legatee gets half, but the relied-upon ruling gives one-third. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and ruling
Note the scenario: a testator with two sons bequeaths to a stranger a share like that of one son. The mufta bihi ruling is that the legatee receives one-third of the estate.
The text states: 'فإن كان له ابنان فله الثلث'
2
Locate the alternative via qiyas
Qiyas would suggest the legatee gets half, because he is like one of the sons. Since there are two sons, each gets half, so the legatee should also get half.
The qiyas is based on equality: the legatee is like each son.
3
Understand why qiyas is overridden
The testator's intent is to make the legatee like one of the sons, not like a co-owner. Making him like one son means his share should not exceed that of a son. With two sons, each gets one-third (since the bequest is taken first, then the residue divided). Thus the legatee gets one-third, aligning with the ceiling that the legatee's share does not exceed a son's share.
The 'first aspect' (وجه الأول) is that the testator intended equality, but the share is capped so it does not exceed a son's share.
4
Note the role of heirs' permission
The qiyas scenario would give half, which exceeds the one-third limit, so heirs' permission ('ijazah') is needed. The mufta bihi gives one-third, which is within the limit, so no permission is required.
The text says 'عند إجازة الورثة' for the qiyas, implying that half requires permission; one-third does not.
5
Apply the tarjih
The Hanafi school adopts the ruling of one-third over the qiyas of half. This is the mufta bihi position, based on the principle that the testator's intent to equalize is fulfilled without exceeding a son's share.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (فإن كان له ابنان فله الثلث والقياس أن يكون له النصف عند إجازة الورثة) لأنه له بمثل نصيب ابنه لكل واحد منهما النصف وجه الأول أنه قصد أن يجعله مثل ابنه إلا أن يزيد نصيبه على نصيب ابنه وذلك بأن يجعل الموصى له كأحدهم.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If he has two sons, then he gets one-third, and the analogy is that he gets one-half with the permission of the heirs) because he has a bequest like the share of his son, each of the two sons gets half. The first aspect is that he intended to make him like his son, except that his share should not exceed the share of his son, and that is by making the legatee like one of them.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This is a ruling from the commentary on the matn. The case: a man with two sons bequeaths to a stranger a share like that of one son. The relied-upon ruling (mufta bihi) is that the legatee takes one-third of the estate. The qiyas would suggest half if the heirs approve, but that is overridden because the testator's intent is to make the legatee like one of the sons, not like a co-owner with them. Since each son gets half of the residue, putting the legatee on par with them would mean each gets one-third, which is the adopted position.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning: comparing the case of two sons to the case of one son to derive a ruling.
إجازة الورثة (ijazat al-wurathah): Permission of the heirs to execute the bequest beyond one-third of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

In Hanafi law, when a testator with two sons bequeaths a share equal to a son's share, the legatee receives one-third, overriding the qiyas that would give half.
